class="nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-cGMI2b"><section class="DA9Qj nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-c5RTEf"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Mandiant </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Written by: Adrian Sanchez Hernandez, Paul Tarter, Ervin James Ocampo</p>
<hr></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>The ransomware-as-a-service (RaaS) model is lowering the barrier of entry into the cybercrime world, causing the number of ransomware attacks we're seeing to spike higher than ever before.</p>
<p>In the last few years, ransomware has become one of the principal sources of income in the cybercrime ecosystem, with increased use of extortion by shaming victims, threatening to release exfiltrated data, and in some cases hitting them with distributed denial-of-service (DDoS) attacks.</p>
<p>This blog post explores activity, similarities and overlaps between multiple ransomware families related to AVADDON ransomware, serving as a case study to understand how ransomware operators think and continue to turn a profit in a constantly evolving cybercrime ecosystem.</p>
<p>Various RaaS services have prevailed in compromising critical targets, leading to major impacts on victim networks and to sizable ransom demands. AVADDON is one of these ransomware services.</p>
<p>The threat actor behind the AVADDON ransomware service started activity in June 2020 and continued operations until June 2021. The service was apparently shut down rapidly—and private encryption keys released—as governments prioritized the fight against ransomware operations with new legislation and increased law enforcement operations.</p>
<p>Mandiant has observed notable overlaps between AVADDON and other ransomware families, indicating the possibility of rebranding in order to reintroduce itself into the RaaS business. Based on the profitability of these operations, it is almost certain that numerous threat actors will continue to conduct ransomware operations.</p>
<h2>Victimology</h2>
<p>Destructive attacks leveraging AVADDON ransomware have <span lang="EN-GB">affected a wide variety of industries. Based on publicly named victims on AVADDON’s own blog post and Mandiant’s incident response investigations, we have observed AVADDON</span> <span lang="EN-GB">impacting industry verticals primarily based in North America, including education, technology, healthcare, financial and government. </span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Based on open-source data, AVADDON released nearly </span><a href="https://threatpost.com/avaddon-ransomware-global-crackdowns/166968/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span lang="EN-GB">3,000 private encryption keys</span></a><span lang="EN-GB">, indicating that targeting is almost certainly broader than directly observed.</span></p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon1_czal.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon1_czal.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon1_czal.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon1_czal.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 1: Industries impacted by AVADDON ransomware based on publicly named victims</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon2_cblg.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon2_cblg.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon2_cblg.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon2_cblg.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 2: Heatmap of publicly named AVADDON victims by country</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h2>AVADDON Ransomware Service</h2>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">In June 2020, Mandiant reported the Russian-speaking threat actor </span>“Avaddon” advertising the AVADDON ransomware affiliate program (RaaS) on the Russian-speaking forums exploit[.]in and xss[.]is. In March 2021, the threat actor Avaddon posted an update for the AVADDON RaaS to announce the development of AVADDON V2 and later that same year incorporated a DDoS feature designed to pressure victims into paying the ransom.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon3_cjxq.max-1400x1400.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon3_cjxq.max-1400x1400.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon3_cjxq.max-1400x1400.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon3_cjxq.max-1400x1400.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 3: AVADDON affiliate panel</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span lang="EN-GB">The AVADDON panel seen in Figure 3, was hosted on a TOR hidden service (.onion domain) and was provided to AVADDON affiliates in order to monitor victims and their payments, track builds, provide a support ticketing system, and view statistics and related news publications. As typical with RaaS operations, AVADDON profits are divided according to their Terms of Service specified by the AVADDON operators, initially structured with 65% profit earned for the affiliate and 35% for the operators of the ransomware service (t</span>his is subject of change by the RaaS service)<span lang="EN-GB">. However, this could vary depending on the number of victims per affiliate, meaning that the profit percentage for the affiliate could be higher if the number of intrusions increased considerably.</span></p>
<h2><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON Affiliates</span></h2>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Mandiant identified multiple AVADDON affiliates involved in targeted destructive attacks leveraging AVADDON ransomware.</span></p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon4_hqjz.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon4_hqjz.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon4_hqjz.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon4_hqjz.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 4: TTPs seen through AVADDON ransomware incidents</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span lang="EN-GB">These attacks employed various tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs), including:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Compromised credentials from prior intrusions. In one instance, Mandiant observed evidence of an AVADDON affiliate working with an initial access broker to gain initial foothold in a victim’s environment.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Custom malware such as BLACKCROW and DARKRAVEN web shells to maintain access and interact with a compromised server.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Publicly available remote administration tool SYSTEMBC for interacting with compromised hosts.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Windows Remote Desktop Protocol (RDP) for lateral movement.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">WDigest downgrade attack in conjunction with SHARPDUMP or MIMIKATZ to dump clear-text credentials from memory.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Open-source frameworks and scripts such as EMPIRE and POWERSPLOIT for post-exploitation activity.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Windows Scheduled Task for persistence.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Publicly available scanning tools such as SoftPerfect Network Scanner for internal reconnaissance.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Publicly available rootkit removal tools such as GMER, PowerTool, and TDSSKiller to disable system protection software.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">ProtonMail service to send and receive data initially at the early stages of the intrusion.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Publicly available archiving tool 7Zip and cloud storage synchronization tool MEGAsync for data staging and exfiltration.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">From the TTPs, Mandiant has observed heavy reliance on open-source and publicly available tooling for different purposes across various stages of the intrusion. </span></p>
<h2><span lang="EN-GB">Connecting the Dots</span></h2>
<p>Mandiant continues to observe sophisticated TTPs and increased collaboration between different and specialized threat actors, each playing a particular role within the intrusion operation and has observed previous cases of rebranding where a new ransomware service aligns with another, suggesting collaboration between ransomware operators.</p>
<p>Mandiant has reported on multiple cases of potential rebranding and links between RaaS such as BLACKMATTER and SABBATH ransomware families and reported on the possibility of rebranding on AVADDON as the RaaS shut down.</p>
<p>Threat actors behind a RaaS may choose rebranding for a variety of reasons including fixing operational security flaws and the perception of reducing the risk of law enforcement disrupting their operations.</p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Mandiant has tracked AVADDON and analyzed its multiple versions along with potential ransomware families with which it shares similarities. In April 2021, Mandiant identified numerous ransomware services advertising in Russian-speaking underground forums, using assorted brands, yet having certain degrees of relations with AVADDON as far back as 2019, as seen in Figure 5.</span></p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon5_agju.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon5_agju.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon5_agju.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon5_agju.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 5: RaaS activity timeline based on forum activity, reported victims and PE timestamps</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span lang="EN-GB">Mandiant has observed previous RaaS services operating since 2019 to 2021 connected to AVADDON source code. The variations among these ransomware families show an increase in maturity as the final product is improved in terms of performance and cryptographic functionality.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">The timeline of these services and the similarities in the code may be part of the rebuilding process, suggesting collaboration between threat actors or access to a shared source code by multiple threat actors.</span></p>
<p>MEDUSALOCKER<span lang="EN-GB"> is ransomware that was advertised on the Russian-speaking forum xss[.]is by the threat actor ‘Scourge’ and reported by </span>Mandiant in October 2019. During 2020, the service continued to be active and was updated with new versions of the ransomware. The last activity from the threat actor in the forum was in February 2021.</p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Ako, also known as MedusaReborn, is ransomware that was initially observed around </span>January 2020<span lang="EN-GB">. Ako operations </span>continued to be active during 2020 and their last publicly exposed victim was added to their shaming blog in July 2020. Mandiant determined that Ako ransomware shares numerous similarities with MEDUSALOCKER and considers Ako to be a variant of MEDUSALOCKER.</p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">ThunderX is a ransomware that started operating in August 2020 according to PE timestamps and </span>public reports<span lang="EN-GB">. However, in September 2020, Tesorion </span>released<span lang="EN-GB"> a free decryptor that appears to have interrupted ThunderX operations. Mandiant determined ThunderX and RANZY to be a single malware family due to numerous similarities and code overlap.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">RANZY, also known as “Ranzy Locker”, is a ransomware that was advertised on the Russian-speaking forums xss[.]is and exploit[.]in by the threat actor “Ranzycorp” in October 2020. RANZY has impacted multiple victims since late 2020, however, Mandiant did not observe publicly named victims on its shaming blog or activity in these forums in 2021.</span></p>
<h2><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON Ransomware Analysis</span></h2>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON encrypts files stored locally and on mapped network shares. It stops targeted processes and services prior to encrypting files. Mandiant has observed three major release versions that can be seen in Figure 5, for further detail on differences in encryption methodology, see the section AVADDON Encryption Evolution.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">The ransomware contains host reconnaissance capabilities to retrieve the system’s Language Code Identifier (LCID) and keyboard layout (Figure 6) and exits if the following languages are found: Russian, Ukrainian, Tatar (Russian) or Sakha (Russian). This is consistent with one of the mandatory policies to which AVADDON affiliates must adhere: the total prohibition of ransomware operations within the Commonwealth of Independent States (CIS) territory.</span></p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon6.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon6.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon6.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon6.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 6: Checks against language and keyboard layout passlist; AVADDON will exit if any of the languages are detected</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON operates similarly to other ransomware samples and contains an embedded configuration. AVADDON’s configuration is stored in the form of global stdstring variables that are initialized prior to main as a C++ global initializer. The strings are decoded as they are needed during execution. All configuration encoding is performed using Base64 and multiple iterations of arithmetic operations utilizing a hardcoded single-byte key that varies per binary. </span>Figure 7<span lang="EN-GB"> is an example python script used for decoding configuration data found in sample hash (MD5:ae663fa3b803d8c23e98373fa3f66d21).</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>from base64 import b64decode

def decode_string(b64_string):
    return &#39;&#39;.join([chr(((x-5)^0xb3)&amp;0xff) for x in b64decode(b64_string)])</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 7: AVADDON string decoding function</div>
</div>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON’s execution flow begins by stopping and deleting services and processes that might interrupt its operation (</span>Figure 8<span lang="EN-GB">). Next, the configuration runs multiple commands that prevent a user from restoring from backup (</span>Table 1<em>)</em><span lang="EN-GB">. Finally, the ransomware begins its encryption operation by recursively iterating local drives and network shares while avoiding directories (</span>Figure 9<span lang="EN-GB">) and files with specific extensions (</span>Figure 10<span lang="EN-GB">).</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON searches the following strings related to virtual machines, backups and antivirus services to stop and delete them:</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>ccEvtMgr 
ccSetMgr 
Culserver 
dbeng8 
dbsrv12 
DefWatch 
Intuit.QuickBooks.FCS 
msmdsrv 
QBCFMonitorService 
QBIDPService 
RTVscan 
SavRoam 
sqladhlp 
SQLADHLP 
sqlagent 
sqlbrowser 
sqlservr 
sqlwriter 
tomcat6 
VMAuthdService 
VMnetDHCP 
VMUSBArbService 
vmware-usbarbitator64 
vmware-usbarbitator64 
VMwareHostd</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 8: Services to stop and delete</div>
</div>

<p><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON deletes the Windows shadow volumes and empties the recycle bin to avoid file recovery. Next, it executes the anti-recovery commands in Figure 9. Finally, to prevent the system from restarting, AVADDON leverages the Windows Restart Manager by adding files actively being encrypted to the Restart Manager registry.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>wmic.exe SHADOWCOPY /nointeractive
wbadmin DELETE SYSTEMSTATEBACKUP
wbadmin DELETE SYSTEMSTATEBACKUP -d
bcdedit.exe /set {default} bootstatuspolicy ignoreallfailures
vssadmin.exe Delete Shadows /All /Quiet</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 9: Anti-recovery commands</div>
</div>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">The following directories are excluded from the encryption process and are consistent across different versions of AVADDON.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>C:\Windows
C:\Program Files
C:\Users\All Users
C:\Users\Public
C:\Users\user\AppData\Local\Temp
C:\Program Files (x86)
C:\Users\user\AppData
C:\ProgramData
C:\Program Files\Microsoft\Exchange Server
C:\Program Files (x86)\Microsoft\Exchange Server
C:\Program Files\Microsoft SQL Server
C:\Program Files (x86)\Microsoft SQL Server</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 10: Directories excluded from encryption</div>
</div>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Additionally, directories containing the following keywords are excluded.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>SYSTEMDRIVE
USERPROFILE
PROGRAMFILES(x86)
ProgramData
Program Files
ALLUSERSPROFILE
AppData
PUBLIC
TMP
Tor Browser
MSOCache
EFI</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 11: Directories containing these keywords are also skipped</div>
</div>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">When encrypting the filesystem, the following extensions are excluded to ensure the system can be recovered after paying the ransom with the provided decryptor.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>.bin
.sys
.ini
.dll
.lnk
.dat
.exe
.drv
.rdp
.prf
.swp

.sql</code></pre>

</div>
<h2><span lang="EN-GB">Host Survey</span></h2>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">The ransomware includes a host survey as part of the ransom note. The host survey is comprised of two parts that are separated by a hyphen character and then Base64 encoded. The first part is a plaintext victim id, the second part is an RSA encrypted JSON structure of data containing the fields in Figure 13.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">

<p>The following is an example JSON string containing the information gathered from the compromised system. This JSON string is not written to disk and is only stored in memory.</p>

<p style="text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%">Figure 14: JSON blob containing host, user and encryption details</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Earlier builds of AVADDON ransomware had a </span><a href="https://gist.github.com/api0cradle/d4aaef39db0d845627d819b2b6b30512" rel="noopener" target="_blank">User Access Control (UAC) bypass module to abuse CMSTPLUA COM interface</a><span lang="EN-GB">. This UAC bypass is common and has been used by multiple ransomwares such as </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/shining-a-light-on-darkside-ransomware-operations" rel="noopener" target="_blank">DARKSIDE</a><span lang="EN-GB"> due to its easy implementation and the availability of public Proof of Concept (PoC) code. </span><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON added modifications over time and removed the UAC bypass.</span></p>
<h2><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON Encryption Evolution</span></h2>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Initially, AVADDON generated a single AES-256 key residing in memory during the encryption process, this key would stay resident until the process exited and was used to encrypt every file. This allowed researchers to develop a decryptor tool released in February 2021 on </span><a href="https://github.com/JavierYuste/AvaddonDecryptor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">GitHub,</a><span lang="EN-GB"> abusing this flaw and dumping the cryptographic materials from the suspended process. Developers of the ransomware fixed this issue by generating a single AES key per file being encrypted.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON, when initially released, used a single AES key for encrypting all files. There are three ways to recover a file’s AES session key:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">The ransom note, as part of the victim ID, requires attackers’ private RSA key.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Each file has a footer appended that contains the session key used to encrypt the file. The key can be extracted with the private RSA key.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Find the session key resident in memory.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON later moved to using a “one key per file” method which resolved the third item of finding a key resident in memory. The other two methods require a private key and are therefore protected. One interesting note: the AES key generated and stored in the ransom note for later versions is never used, only the session keys stored in the file are correct, this is remnant of moving to a “one key per file” methodology vs a single key that could be stored once.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Analysis across AVADDON samples containing the file encryption fix showed the replacement of the used C++ structures for global variables indicating a quick fix was made by the threat actors developing AVADDON.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Samples observed later in 2021 with PE timestamps of April 2021 include improvements such as the addition of IOCompletionPort multithreading and the replacement of these global variables again for structures just like the original release.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">This indicates that the threat actor implemented a quick fix after the release of the PoC decryptor to continue with the operations while improved and more consistent versions of the software were under development.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Additionally, latest versions of AVADDON had a function to force the restart of the victim host in safemode prior to encrypting the filesystem by providing the command line parameter “-safe” to the encryptor executable; mimicking other RaaS services that implemented similar features in attempt to bypass Antivirus and EDR software.</span></p>
<h2><span lang="EN-GB">Ransomware Similarities and Technical Comparison</span></h2>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Mandiant analyzed multiple samples of MEDUSALOCKER, Ako, ThunderX, AVADDON and RANZY, finding multiple similarities between these ransomwares including the programming language, code similarities and functionalities, many of these can be observed in Figure 15.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Additionally, there’s a significant overlap in their infrastructure as Ako and RANZY used the same Tor onion address 7rckgo66iydpvgpwve7b2el5q2zhjw4tv4lmyewufnpx4lhkekxkoqd[.]onion.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">This Tor site was used for the purposes of shaming victims and coercing them to pay ransom demands, showing another degree of relationship between them.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Similar PDB strings have been seen across multiple samples of MEDUSALOCKER, ThunderX (MD5: fedadfa6ce199900ca0a6a1f588f8beb), RANZY (MD5: d3774470f4a1b2451fb314d6c37a7763) and in a sample of AVADDON decryptor (MD5: 01422fd3eec0d1a0ce238df01edf8a50) contributing to show potential links between them.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Note that both ThunderX and RANZ</span><span lang="EN-GB">Y share the same PDB string.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Example PDB paths:</span></p>
<ul>
<li>C:\Users\Gh0St\Desktop\MedusaLockerInfo\MedusaLockerProject\MedusaLocker\Release\MedusaLocker.pdb</li>
<li>C:\Users\Gh0St\Desktop\MedusaLockerInfo\MedusaLockerProject\MedusaLocker\Debug\MedusaLockerXP.pdb</li>
<li>C:\Users\Gh0St\Desktop\MedusaLockerInfo\MedusaLockerProject\MedusaLocker\Release\MedusaLockerXP.pdb</li>
<li>C:\Users\Gh0St\Desktop\MedusaLockerInfo\MedusaLockerProject\MedusaLocker\Debug\MedusaLocker.pdb</li>
<li>C:\Users\Gh0St\Desktop\MedusaLockerInfo\MedusaLockerProject_v2\MedusaLocker\Debug\MedusaLocker.pdb</li>
<li>C:\Users\Gh0St\Desktop\AvaddonProjectInfo\AvaddonProject\AvaddonLockerLite\Release\AvaddonUnlockerGlobalLiteXPStub.pdb</li>
<li>C:\Users\Gh0St\Desktop\ThunderX\Release\LockerStub.pdb</li>
</ul>

<p style="text-align:center"><span lang="EN-GB" style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%">Figure 15: Table showing similarities between ransomware</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">*Ako (MEDUSALOCKER variant)</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">*ThunderX (RANZY variant)</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">Encryption Operations</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Code reuse appeared very similar throughout initializing a cryptographic context for all of them. After analyzing each ransomware extensively, many functions had the same architecture and similar structure for storage and were found to always be evident and easy to pick out once reversing a sample.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Between the five ransomware, there are four modes of file encryption supported describing how much of the file to encrypt and in one case wiping the contents of the file instead of encrypting. These modes are the same across each ransomware.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">File Signature</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">MEDUSALOCKER initially did not use file signature to append to the encrypted files, however Ako appends the hex value 0xBEEFCACE.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON appends the hex value 0x1030307 to the end of the file within a 24-byte structure.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">RANZY and ThunderX use the signature hex value 0xB0E0E0F0.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">These signatures are part of a 24-byte structure that is very similar across all of them, prior to this footer is the file’s session key encrypted with the attacker’s public key.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">Identifier Format</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Ako, ThunderX and RANZY construct highly similar JSON blobs that are encoded and written to the ransom notes. These contain multiple keywords that overlap between them.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Ako and AVADDON written identifiers contain similarities and overlap some keywords, however, AVADDON constructs a larger JSON (seen in Figure 14) and uses additional keywords not present in Ako.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">Obfuscation</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">MEDUSALOCKER and Ako contain plain text strings and only the RSA key blob is base64 encoded. AVADDON, ThunderX and RANZY have different implementations to obfuscate both the RSA key and the strings.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">UAC Bypass</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">MEDUSALOCKER and AVADDON use a similar UAC bypass functionality abusing the CMSTPLUA COM interface while ThunderX and RANZY don’t include a bypass.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">Backup Deletion</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">The ransomware families delete backups by executing very similar command arguments to delete the Windows Shadow Copies invoking wbadmin, bcedit.exe and vssadmin.exe and</span><span lang="EN-GB"> to empty the recycle bin.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">Service and Process Enumeration</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">These pieces of ransomware use a remarkably similar list of process and services to scan and stop if found and following a similar order. However, AVADDON contains more processes and services related to VMWare, Antivirus, SQLServer and Tomcat services and processes.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">ThunderX and RANZY have different process and services lists and only some overlap. Additionally, the list of services to stop is considerably shorter than the rest of the ransomware, while the list of processes is larger.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">File and Directory Exclusions</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">MEDUSALOCKER and AVADDON contain nearly identical file and directory exclusions, however, AVADDON contains additional directories that are skipped including TOR browser directories and a shorter list of file extensions skipped from the encryption process.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">Network Scanning</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">MEDUSALOCKER, Ako, ThunderX and RANZY contain similar code to scan the victim’s network by using IcmpSendEcho function.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">First builds identified for AVADDON rely on SendARP function, however, some of the AVADDON samples with PE timestamps from February 2021 show a replacement in the network reconnaissance module to use the IcmpCreateFile and IcmpSendEcho functions.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">Other Differences</span></h3>
<ul>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">Different versions of AVADDON have added exclusion folders and it can receive a CLI argument.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-GB">ThunderX and RANZY can receive the CLI argument –nolan.</span></li>
</ul>
<h2><span lang="EN-GB">Conclusion</span></h2>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">The similarities between these ransomware families and the timespan of activity suggest that the MEDUSALOCKER code base was likely reused by different threat actors that started operating RaaS in 2020. The binary similarities and techniques used show a certain degree of relationship between them. However, the technical skills of each threat actor involved in the development of these RaaS and the implementations vary per ransomware.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON constitutes a considerable evolution compared to MEDUSALOCKER, however, it is unclear whether AVADDON was the successor of MEDUSALOCKER due to technical differences and overlap in their activity. The AVADDON service was operational for about a year and gained considerable popularity among threat actors to extort victims across multiple regions and industries until events forced a fast exit followed by the shutdown of its operations and the release of the victim's private keys.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Given the similarities suggesting code overlap between these ransomware families, the different skillsets of the involved operators and the overlap in time, it is unclear whether the Law Enforcement disruption of ransomware operations at this time will be definitive for the threat actors responsible for AVADDON.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-GB">Rebranding, code sharing, or the purchase of source code by different threat actors are practical possibilities to get back into extortion operations as seen in previous cases such as SODINOKIBI’s </span>connections with GANDCRAB ransomware, or the rebuild and rebrand of a new ransomware using another’s code base such as BLACKMATTER<span lang="EN-GB">.</span></p>

<h2><span lang="EN-GB">Appendix: Malware definitions</span></h2>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">AVADDON</span></h3>
<p>AVADDON is ransomware written in C++ that encrypts files stored locally and on mapped network shares. AVADDON terminates targeted processes and services prior to encrypting files. AVADDON uses the AES-256 encryption algorithm to encrypt files. AVADDON is a Windows ransomware written in C++. It is run as an affiliate program, RaaS. System files are encrypted using a single AES-256 key. The AES-256 key is generated at run-time and encrypted using an embedded RSA public key. The malware also has anti-analysis techniques and check that it is not running in a system with Russian language.</p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">MEDUSALOCKER</span></h3>
<p>MEDUSALOCKER is ransomware written in C++ that encrypts files stored locally and on network shares with a randomly generated AES-256 key. MEDUSALOCKER can bypass User Account Control (UAC) and terminates targeted processes and services prior to encrypting files. Persistence is established using a scheduled task. MEDUSALOCKER empties the Recycle Bin and delete volume shadow copies.</p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">RANZY</span></h3>
<p>RANZY is a ransomware written in C++ that encrypts files stored locally and on mapped network shares appending the extension "[.]ranzy" to them. RANZY uses Salsa20 stream cipher to encrypt files.</p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">DARKRAVEN</span></h3>
<p>DARKRAVEN is an ASPX webshell written in C# that functions as a backdoor. The backdoor supports shell command execution and file transfer. Supplied data is decrypted and decoded using 3DES and a custom Base64 decoding routine.</p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">BLACKCROW</span></h3>
<p>BLACKCROW is an ASPX webshell written in VB[.]NET that functions as backdoor. BLACKCROW expects an attacker to upload a [.]NET DLL payload alongside additional arguments. These arguments are used to invoke a series of methods within the DLL. The results of the final invocation are displayed to the attacker.</p>
<h3><span lang="EN-GB">SYSTEMBC</span></h3>
<p>SYSTEMBC is a tunneler written in C that retrieves proxy-related commands from a C2 server using a custom binary protocol over TCP. A C2 server directs SYSTEMBC to act as a proxy between the C2 server and a remote system. SYSTEMBC is also capable of retrieving additional payloads via HTTP. Some variants may utilize the Tor network for this purpose. Downloaded payloads may be written to disk or mapped directly into memory prior to execution. SYSTEMBC is often utilized to hide network traffic associated with other malware families. Observed families include DANABOT, SMOKELOADER, and URSNIF.</p>

Ocampo\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003chr\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eThe ransomware-as-a-service (RaaS) model is lowering the barrier of entry into the cybercrime world, causing the number of ransomware attacks we're seeing to spike higher than ever before.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn the last few years, ransomware has become one of the principal sources of income in the cybercrime ecosystem, with increased use of extortion by shaming victims, threatening to release exfiltrated data, and in some cases hitting them with distributed denial-of-service (DDoS) attacks.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis blog post explores activity, similarities and overlaps between multiple ransomware families related to AVADDON ransomware, serving as a case study to understand how ransomware operators think and continue to turn a profit in a constantly evolving cybercrime ecosystem.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eVarious RaaS services have prevailed in compromising critical targets, leading to major impacts on victim networks and to sizable ransom demands. AVADDON is one of these ransomware services.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe threat actor behind the AVADDON ransomware service started activity in June 2020 and continued operations until June 2021. The service was apparently shut down rapidly—and private encryption keys released—as governments prioritized the fight against ransomware operations with new legislation and increased law enforcement operations.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant has observed notable overlaps between AVADDON and other ransomware families, indicating the possibility of rebranding in order to reintroduce itself into the RaaS business. Based on the profitability of these operations, it is almost certain that numerous threat actors will continue to conduct ransomware operations.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eVictimology\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eDestructive attacks leveraging AVADDON ransomware have \u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eaffected a wide variety of industries. Based on publicly named victims on AVADDON’s own blog post and Mandiant’s incident response investigations, we have observed AVADDON\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eimpacting industry verticals primarily based in North America, including education, technology, healthcare, financial and government. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eBased on open-source data, AVADDON released nearly \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://threatpost.com/avaddon-ransomware-global-crackdowns/166968/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e3,000 private encryption keys\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e, indicating that targeting is almost certainly broader than directly observed.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 1: Industries impacted by AVADDON ransomware based on publicly named victims\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon1_czal.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon1_czal.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 2: Heatmap of publicly named AVADDON victims by country\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon2_cblg.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon2_cblg.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch2\u003eAVADDON Ransomware Service\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eIn June 2020, Mandiant reported the Russian-speaking threat actor \u003c/span\u003e“Avaddon” advertising the AVADDON ransomware affiliate program (RaaS) on the Russian-speaking forums exploit[.]in and xss[.]is. In March 2021, the threat actor Avaddon posted an update for the AVADDON RaaS to announce the development of AVADDON V2 and later that same year incorporated a DDoS feature designed to pressure victims into paying the ransom.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 3: AVADDON affiliate panel\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon3_cjxq.max-1400x1400.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon3_cjxq.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon3_cjxq.max-1400x1400.png 1359w"," 1060px,  1359px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon3_cjxq.max-1400x1400.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThe AVADDON panel seen in Figure 3, was hosted on a TOR hidden service (.onion domain) and was provided to AVADDON affiliates in order to monitor victims and their payments, track builds, provide a support ticketing system, and view statistics and related news publications. As typical with RaaS operations, AVADDON profits are divided according to their Terms of Service specified by the AVADDON operators, initially structured with 65% profit earned for the affiliate and 35% for the operators of the ransomware service (t\u003c/span\u003ehis is subject of change by the RaaS service)\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e. However, this could vary depending on the number of victims per affiliate, meaning that the profit percentage for the affiliate could be higher if the number of intrusions increased considerably.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON Affiliates\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eMandiant identified multiple AVADDON affiliates involved in targeted destructive attacks leveraging AVADDON ransomware.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 4: TTPs seen through AVADDON ransomware incidents\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon4_hqjz.max-1200x1200.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon4_hqjz.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon4_hqjz.max-1200x1200.png 1166w"," 1060px,  1166px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon4_hqjz.max-1200x1200.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThese attacks employed various tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs), including:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eCompromised credentials from prior intrusions. In one instance, Mandiant observed evidence of an AVADDON affiliate working with an initial access broker to gain initial foothold in a victim’s environment.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eCustom malware such as BLACKCROW and DARKRAVEN web shells to maintain access and interact with a compromised server.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003ePublicly available remote administration tool SYSTEMBC for interacting with compromised memory.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eOpen-source frameworks and scripts such as EMPIRE and POWERSPLOIT for post-exploitation activity.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eWindows Scheduled Task for persistence.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003ePublicly available scanning tools such as SoftPerfect Network Scanner for internal reconnaissance.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003ePublicly available rootkit removal tools such as GMER, PowerTool, and TDSSKiller to disable system protection software.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eProtonMail service to send and receive data initially at the early stages of the intrusion.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003ePublicly available archiving tool 7Zip and cloud storage synchronization tool MEGAsync for data staging and exfiltration.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eFrom the TTPs, Mandiant has observed heavy reliance on open-source and publicly available tooling for different purposes across various stages of the intrusion. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eConnecting the Dots\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant continues to observe sophisticated TTPs and increased collaboration between different and specialized threat actors, each playing a particular role within the intrusion operation and has observed previous cases of rebranding where a new ransomware service aligns with another, suggesting collaboration between ransomware operators.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant has reported on multiple cases of potential rebranding and links between RaaS such as BLACKMATTER and SABBATH ransomware families and reported on the possibility of rebranding on AVADDON as the RaaS shut down.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThreat actors behind a RaaS may choose rebranding for a variety of reasons including fixing operational security flaws and the perception of reducing the risk of law enforcement disrupting their operations.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eMandiant has tracked AVADDON and analyzed its multiple versions along with potential ransomware families with which it shares similarities. In April 2021, Mandiant identified numerous ransomware services advertising in Russian-speaking underground forums, using assorted brands, yet having certain degrees of relations with AVADDON as far back as 2019, as seen in Figure 5.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 5: RaaS activity timeline based on forum activity, reported victims and PE timestamps\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon5_agju.max-1100x1100.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon5_agju.max-1100x1100.png 1010w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon5_agju.max-1100x1100.png 1010w"," 1010px,  1010px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon5_agju.max-1100x1100.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eMandiant has observed previous RaaS services operating since 2019 to 2021 connected to AVADDON source code. The variations among these ransomware families show an increase in maturity as the final product is improved in terms of performance and cryptographic functionality.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThe timeline of these services and the similarities in the code may be part of the rebuilding process, suggesting collaboration between threat actors or access to a shared source code by multiple threat actors.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMEDUSALOCKER\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e is ransomware that was advertised on the Russian-speaking forum xss[.]is by the threat actor ‘Scourge’ and reported by \u003c/span\u003eMandiant in October 2019. During 2020, the service continued to be active and was updated with new versions of the ransomware. The last activity from the threat actor in the forum was in February 2021.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAko, also known as MedusaReborn, is ransomware that was initially observed around \u003c/span\u003eJanuary 2020\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e. Ako operations \u003c/span\u003econtinued to be active during 2020 and their last publicly exposed victim was added to their shaming blog in July 2020. Mandiant determined that Ako ransomware shares numerous similarities with MEDUSALOCKER and considers Ako to be a variant of MEDUSALOCKER.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThunderX is a ransomware that started operating in August 2020 according to PE timestamps and \u003c/span\u003epublic reports\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e. However, in September 2020, Tesorion \u003c/span\u003ereleased\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e a free decryptor that appears to have interrupted ThunderX operations. Mandiant determined ThunderX and RANZY to be a single malware family due to numerous similarities and code overlap.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eRANZY, also known as “Ranzy Locker”, is a ransomware that was advertised on the Russian-speaking forums xss[.]is and exploit[.]in by the threat actor “Ranzycorp” in October 2020. RANZY has impacted multiple victims since late 2020, however, Mandiant did not observe publicly named victims on its shaming blog or activity in these forums in 2021.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON Ransomware Analysis\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON encrypts files stored locally and on mapped network shares. It stops targeted processes and services prior to encrypting files. Mandiant has observed three major release versions that can be seen in Figure 5, for further detail on differences in encryption methodology, see the section AVADDON Encryption Evolution.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThe ransomware contains host reconnaissance capabilities to retrieve the system’s Language Code Identifier (LCID) and keyboard layout (Figure 6) and exits if the following languages are found: Russian, Ukrainian, Tatar (Russian) or Sakha (Russian). This is consistent with one of the mandatory policies to which AVADDON affiliates must adhere: the total prohibition of ransomware operations within the Commonwealth of Independent States (CIS) territory.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 6: Checks against language and keyboard layout passlist; AVADDON will exit if any of the languages are detected\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon6.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/avaddon6.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON operates similarly to other ransomware samples and contains an embedded configuration. AVADDON’s configuration is stored in the form of global stdstring variables that are initialized prior to main as a C++ global initializer. The strings are decoded as they are needed during execution. All configuration encoding is performed using Base64 and multiple iterations of arithmetic operations utilizing a hardcoded single-byte key that varies per binary. \u003c/span\u003eFigure 7\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e is an example python script used for decoding configuration data found in sample hash (MD5:ae663fa3b803d8c23e98373fa3f66d21).\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003efrom base64 import b64decode\r\n\r\ndef decode_string(b64_string):\r\n    return \u0026#39;\u0026#39;.join([chr(((x-5)^0xb3)\u0026amp;0xff) for x in b64decode(b64_string)])\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center\"\u003eFigure 7: AVADDON string decoding function\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON’s execution flow begins by stopping and deleting services and processes that might interrupt its operation (\u003c/span\u003eFigure 8\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e). Next, the configuration runs multiple commands that prevent a user from restoring from backup (\u003c/span\u003eTable 1\u003cem\u003e)\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e. Finally, the ransomware begins its encryption operation by recursively iterating local drives and network shares while avoiding directories (\u003c/span\u003eFigure 9\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e) and files with specific extensions (\u003c/span\u003eFigure 10\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e).\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON searches the following strings related to virtual machines, backups and antivirus services to stop and delete them:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eccEvtMgr \r\nccSetMgr \r\nCulserver \r\ndbeng8 \r\ndbsrv12 \r\nDefWatch \r\nIntuit.QuickBooks.FCS \r\nmsmdsrv \r\nQBCFMonitorService \r\nQBIDPService \r\nRTVscan \r\nSavRoam \r\nsqladhlp \r\nSQLADHLP \r\nsqlagent \r\nsqlbrowser \r\nsqlservr \r\nsqlwriter \r\ntomcat6 \r\nVMAuthdService \r\nVMnetDHCP \r\nVMUSBArbService \r\nvmware-usbarbitator64 \r\nvmware-usbarbitator64 \r\nVMwareHostd\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center\"\u003eFigure 8: Services to stop and delete\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThe following processes are stopped. The ransomware stores a short-hand form of the full process name, but it does not appear to be used during process identification or The host survey is comprised of two parts that are separated by a hyphen character and then Base64 encoded. 13.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"text-align:center\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%\"\u003eFigure 14: JSON blob containing host, user and encryption details\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eEarlier builds of AVADDON ransomware had a \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://gist.github.com/api0cradle/d4aaef39db0d845627d819b2b6b30512\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eUser Access Control (UAC) bypass module to abuse CMSTPLUA COM interface\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e. This UAC bypass is common and has been used by multiple ransomwares such as \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/shining-a-light-on-darkside-ransomware-operations\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eDARKSIDE\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e due to its easy implementation and the availability of public Proof of Concept (PoC) code. \u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON added modifications over time and removed the UAC bypass.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON Encryption Evolution\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eInitially, AVADDON generated a single AES-256 key residing in memory during the encryption process, this key would stay resident until the process exited and was used to encrypt every file. This allowed researchers to develop a decryptor tool released in February 2021 on \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/JavierYuste/AvaddonDecryptor\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eGitHub,\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e abusing this flaw and dumping the cryptographic materials from the suspended process. Developers of the ransomware fixed this issue by generating a single AES key per file being encrypted.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON, when initially released, used a single AES key for encrypting all files. There are three ways to recover a file’s AES session key:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThe ransom note, as part of the victim ID, requires attackers’ private RSA key.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eEach file has a footer appended that contains the session key used to encrypt the file. The key can be extracted with the private RSA key.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eFind the session key resident in memory.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON later moved to using a “one key per file” method which resolved the third item of finding a key resident in memory. The other two methods require a private key and are therefore protected. One interesting note: the AES key generated and stored in the ransom note for later versions is never used, only the session keys stored in the file are correct, this is remnant of moving to a “one key per file” methodology vs a single key that could be stored once.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAnalysis across AVADDON samples containing the file encryption fix showed the replacement of the used C++ structures for global variables indicating a quick fix was made by the threat actors developing AVADDON.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eSamples observed later in 2021 with PE timestamps of April 2021 include improvements such as the addition of IOCompletionPort multithreading and the replacement of these global variables again for structures just like the original release.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThis indicates that the threat actor implemented a quick fix after the release of the PoC decryptor to continue with the operations while improved and more consistent versions of the software were under development.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAdditionally, latest versions of AVADDON had a function to force the restart of the victim host in safemode prior to encrypting the filesystem by providing the command line parameter “-safe” to the encryptor executable; mimicking other RaaS services that implemented similar features in attempt to bypass Antivirus and EDR software.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eRansomware Similarities and Technical Comparison\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eMandiant analyzed multiple samples of MEDUSALOCKER, Ako, ThunderX, AVADDON and RANZY, finding multiple similarities between these ransomwares including the programming language, code similarities and functionalities, many of these can be observed in Figure 15.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAdditionally, there’s a significant overlap in their infrastructure as Ako and RANZY used the same Tor onion address 7rckgo66iydpvgpwve7b2el5q2zhjw4tv4lmyewufnpx4lhkekxkoqd[.]onion.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThis Tor site was used for the purposes of shaming victims and coercing them to pay ransom demands, showing another degree of relationship between them.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eSimilar PDB strings have been seen across multiple samples of MEDUSALOCKER, ThunderX (MD5: fedadfa6ce199900ca0a6a1f588f8beb), RANZY (MD5: d3774470f4a1b2451fb314d6c37a7763) and in a sample of AVADDON decryptor (MD5: 01422fd3eec0d1a0ce238df01edf8a50) contributing to show potential links between them.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eNote that both ThunderX and RANZ\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eY share the same PDB string.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eExample PDB style\u003d\"text-align:center\"\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\" style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%\"\u003eFigure 15: Table showing similarities between ransomware\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e*Ako (MEDUSALOCKER variant)\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e*ThunderX (RANZY variant)\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eEncryption Operations\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eCode reuse appeared very similar throughout initializing a cryptographic context for all of them. After analyzing each ransomware extensively, many functions had the same architecture and similar structure for storage and were found to always be evident and easy to pick out once reversing a sample.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eBetween the five ransomware, there are four modes of file encryption supported describing how much of the file to encrypt and in one case wiping the contents of the file instead of encrypting. These modes are the same across each ransomware.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eFile Signature\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eMEDUSALOCKER initially did not use file signature to append to the encrypted files, however Ako appends the hex value 0xBEEFCACE.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON appends the hex value 0x1030307 to the end of the file within a 24-byte structure.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eRANZY and ThunderX use the signature hex value 0xB0E0E0F0.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThese signatures are part of a 24-byte structure that is very similar across all of them, prior to this footer is the file’s session key encrypted with the attacker’s public key.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eIdentifier Format\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAko, ThunderX and RANZY construct highly similar JSON blobs that are encoded and written to the ransom notes. These contain multiple keywords that overlap between them.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAko and AVADDON written identifiers contain similarities and overlap some keywords, however, AVADDON constructs a larger JSON (seen in Figure 14) and uses additional keywords not present in Ako.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eObfuscation\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eMEDUSALOCKER and Ako contain plain text strings and only the RSA key blob is base64 encoded. AVADDON, ThunderX and RANZY have different implementations to obfuscate both the RSA key and the strings.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eUAC Bypass\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eMEDUSALOCKER and AVADDON use a similar UAC bypass functionality abusing the CMSTPLUA COM interface while ThunderX and RANZY don’t include a bypass.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eBackup Deletion\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThe ransomware families delete backups by executing very similar command arguments to delete the Windows Shadow Copies invoking wbadmin, bcedit.exe and vssadmin.exe and\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e to empty the recycle bin.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eService and Process Enumeration\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThese pieces of ransomware use a remarkably similar list of process and services to scan and stop if found and following a similar order. However, AVADDON contains more processes and services related to VMWare, Antivirus, SQLServer and Tomcat services and processes.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThunderX and RANZY have different process and services lists and only some overlap. Additionally, the list of services to stop is considerably shorter than the rest of the ransomware, while the list of processes is larger.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eFile and Directory Exclusions\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eMEDUSALOCKER and AVADDON contain nearly identical file and directory exclusions, however, AVADDON contains additional directories that are skipped including TOR browser directories and a shorter list of file extensions skipped from the encryption process.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eNetwork Scanning\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eMEDUSALOCKER, Ako, ThunderX and RANZY contain similar code to scan the victim’s network by using IcmpSendEcho function.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eFirst builds identified for AVADDON rely on SendARP function, however, some of the AVADDON samples with PE timestamps from February 2021 show a replacement in the network reconnaissance module to use the IcmpCreateFile and IcmpSendEcho functions.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eOther Differences\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eDifferent versions of AVADDON have added exclusion folders and it can receive a CLI argument.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThunderX and RANZY can receive the CLI argument –nolan.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eConclusion\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eThe similarities between these ransomware families and the timespan of activity suggest that the MEDUSALOCKER code base was likely reused by different threat actors that started operating RaaS in 2020. The binary similarities and techniques used show a certain degree of relationship between them. However, the technical skills of each threat actor involved in the development of these RaaS and the implementations vary per ransomware.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON constitutes a considerable evolution compared to MEDUSALOCKER, however, it is unclear whether AVADDON was the successor of MEDUSALOCKER due to technical differences and overlap in their activity. The AVADDON service was operational for about a year and gained considerable popularity among threat actors to extort victims across multiple regions and industries until events forced a fast exit followed by the shutdown of its operations and the release of the victim's private keys.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eGiven the similarities suggesting code overlap between these ransomware families, the different skillsets of the involved operators and the overlap in time, it is unclear whether the Law Enforcement disruption of ransomware operations at this time will be definitive for the threat actors responsible for AVADDON.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eRebranding, code sharing, or the purchase of source code by different threat actors are practical possibilities to get back into extortion operations as seen in previous cases such as SODINOKIBI’s \u003c/span\u003econnections with GANDCRAB ransomware, or the rebuild and rebrand of a new ransomware using another’s code base such as BLACKMATTER\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003e.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAppendix: ATT\u0026amp;CK Mapping\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cdiv #000000;padding:16px;width:82.8877%\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eProtected Theater - WMIC Shadowcopy Delete, Variant #1\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAppendix: Malware definitions\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAVADDON\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAVADDON is ransomware written in C++ that encrypts files stored locally and on mapped network shares. AVADDON terminates targeted processes and services prior to encrypting files. AVADDON uses the AES-256 encryption algorithm to encrypt files. AVADDON is a Windows ransomware written in C++. It is run as an affiliate program, RaaS. System files are encrypted using a single AES-256 key. The AES-256 key is generated at run-time and encrypted using an embedded RSA public key. The malware also has anti-analysis techniques and check that it is not running in a system with Russian language.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eMEDUSALOCKER\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMEDUSALOCKER is ransomware written in C++ that encrypts files stored locally and on network shares with a randomly generated AES-256 key. MEDUSALOCKER can bypass User Account Control (UAC) and terminates targeted processes and services prior to encrypting files. Persistence is established using a scheduled task. MEDUSALOCKER empties the Recycle Bin and delete volume shadow copies.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eRANZY\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eRANZY is a ransomware written in C++ that encrypts files stored locally and on mapped network shares appending the extension \"[.]ranzy\" to them. RANZY uses Salsa20 stream cipher to encrypt files.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eDARKRAVEN\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eDARKRAVEN is an ASPX webshell written in C# that functions as a backdoor. The backdoor supports shell command execution and file transfer. Supplied data is decrypted and decoded using 3DES and a custom Base64 decoding routine.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eBLACKCROW\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBLACKCROW is an ASPX webshell written in VB[.]NET that functions as backdoor. BLACKCROW expects an attacker to upload a [.]NET DLL payload alongside additional arguments. These arguments are used to invoke a series of methods within the DLL. The results of the final invocation are displayed to the attacker.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eSYSTEMBC\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSYSTEMBC is a tunneler written in C that retrieves proxy-related commands from a C2 server using a custom binary protocol over TCP. A C2 server directs SYSTEMBC to act as a proxy between the C2 server and a remote system. SYSTEMBC is also capable of retrieving additional payloads via HTTP. Some variants may utilize the Tor network for this purpose. Downloaded payloads may be written to disk or mapped directly into memory prior to execution. SYSTEMBC is often utilized to hide network traffic associated with other malware families. Observed families include DANABOT, SMOKELOADER, and URSNIF.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAcknowledgements\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-GB\"\u003eAdvanced Practices team, FLARE team, Threat Pursuit team and Yash Gupta\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e"]]]],[["Threat Intelligence","BitM Up!
